Finding Semen on Garment: Repetition of Salah
Ruling from Ibn Rustam's Nawadir on the obligation to repeat Salah based on finding semen vs blood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling regarding repetition of Salah when semen or blood is found on a garment, and explain the rationale based on likelihood and cause.

Core Idea
If a man finds semen on his garment, he must repeat Salah from his last wet dream; blood requires no repetition.

This branch ruling from Ibn Rustam's Nawadir addresses a practical scenario: a man wakes up to find a spot on his garment, uncertain when it occurred. The ruling distinguishes between semen and blood based on the likelihood of the source being his own impurity. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the principle of certainty and reasonable assumption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two substances
Distinguish between semen (maniyy) and blood (dam) as the impurities found.
Both are najis in Hanafi school, but the ruling differs.
2
State the ruling for each
For semen: repeat Salah from the last wet dream (ihtilam). For blood: no repetition.
The ruling is attributed to Abu Hanifa via Nawadir.
3
Understand the rationale
Semen is presumed to be his own because it comes from him; blood could be from someone else (e.g., contact with another person's blood) and thus uncertain.
The principle: certainty is required for obligation; uncertainty prevents it.
4
Note the time frame
Repetition starts from the last time he had a wet dream (ihtilam), not from an arbitrary point.
The cause (ihtilam) marks the earliest possible time of impurity.
5
Recognize the authority level
This ruling is from Ibn Rustam's Nawadir, a non-Zahir al-Riwayah source, hence of lower authority in the Hanafi school.
It is a transmitted position but may not be the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فروع: ذكر ابن رستم في نوادره عن أبي حنيفة من وجد في ثوبه منياً أعاد من آخر ما احتلم، وإن كان دماً لا يعيد لأن دم غيره قد يصيبه والظاهر أن الإصابة لم تتقدم زمان يصيب ثوبه فالظاهر أنه منيه فيعتبر وجوده من وقت وجود سبب فأما وجوده، مني غيره لا . والثاني) وهما إن توضؤوا منها وهم محدثون أو اغتسلوا من جنابة. قوله : (وأما مسألة النجاسة) أي المذكورة في دليل الإمامين قوله: (وفي المجتبى وحكم ما عجن به حكم الوضوء والغسل) ينظر ما هذا العجين والثوب إذا غسل لا عن نجاسة حيث حكم في العجين بتنجسه دون الثوب. الفرق بين
Branches: Ibn Rustam mentioned in his Nawadir from Abu Hanifa: Whoever finds semen on his garment, he repeats (Salah) from the last time he had a wet dream; and if it is blood, he does not repeat, because blood of others may have touched him, and the apparent is that the touch did not precede the time it touched his garment, so the apparent is that it is his semen, so its existence is considered from the time of the cause of its existence. But if it is someone else's semen, then no. And the second: They are if they made wudhu from it while in need of wudhu or made ghusl from janaba. His statement: (And as for the issue of impurity) meaning what was mentioned in the proof of the two Imams. His statement: (And in al-Mujtaba and the ruling of what is kneaded with it is like the ruling of wudhu and ghusl) Look: what is this dough and the garment washed without impurity? It is ruled that the dough is impure unlike the garment. The difference between...
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents a branch ruling (فروع) from Ibn Rustam's al-Nawadir, attributed to Abu Hanifa. The ruling: If a man finds semen on his garment, he must repeat his Salah from the last time he had a wet dream (ihtilam), because the presence of semen indicates that it is likely his own, and the time of its occurrence is assumed to be from the most recent possible cause. However, if he finds blood on his garment, he does not need to repeat prayers, because it could be blood from someone else (like an injury from a stranger) and the timing is uncertain. There is also a mention of the two scenarios (already discussed: making wudhu/ghusl from the well water). Then the text quotes a statement from the matn about the ruling of dough (عجين) being like wudhu/ghusl, and questions why dough becomes impure while a garment washed with the same water is not impure. This hints at the difference between water acting as purifier and water being mixed with dough. The passage ends abruptly with 'the difference between'.

Key Terms
احتلم (iḥtalam): Had a wet dream; the legal time from which repetition of Salah is required if semen is found on clothes.
مني (maniyy): Semen; a najas (impure) substance that requires ghusl and invalidates prayer if on the body or clothes during salah.
نوادر (nawādir): Transmission of rare or uncommon narrations within the Hanafi school, not part of the six fundamental books (Zahir al-Riwayah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the ruling in this lesson, if a man finds semen on his garment, from what point must he repeat his prayers?
A) From the last time he performed ghusl.
B) From the last time he had a wet dream.
C) From the beginning of the night.
D) He need not repeat if he is unsure.

Key Takeaway
The obligation to repeat past Salah when finding semen depends on the presumption that it is one's own and relates to the time of the last possible cause (ihtilam); blood does not trigger such obligation due to uncertainty.

Time Limit for Najasah in a Well via Apparent Cause
Explaining time limit via apparent cause principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of attributing an effect to its apparent cause (sabab zahir) to derive the time limit for najasah in a well when the time of impurity is unknown.

Core Idea
When the cause is hidden, rely on apparent signs like bloating to estimate time.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains how Hanafi jurists derive the time limit for najasah in a well when a dead animal falls in at an unknown time. Using qiyas from murder cases, they establish a legal presumption based on physical signs. Understanding this analogy reveals the school's method of resolving uncertainty through apparent causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Recognize the legal problem: a dead animal falls into a well, making the water najis. The time of fall is unknown. The ruling is that prayers performed during that period must be repeated. Determine whether the period is three days (if the animal is bloated) or one day and night (if not).
Bloating indicates decomposition, which takes about three days; absence indicates recent fall.
2
Locate the dalil
The commentator uses qiyas (analogy) from murder cases. In Hanafi law, if a person is wounded and later dies, the death is attributed to the wound even if another cause is possible, because the wound is the apparent cause. Similarly, for a well, bloating is the apparent cause for a three-day period.
The asl (original case) is murder attribution; the far' (new case) is najasah time estimation.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The connecting factor (illa) is that the true cause is hidden, so we must refer to an observable sign. In murder, the wound or locality is the sign; in najasah, bloating (or its absence) is the sign. The ruling follows the sign.
Hanafi usul: when the musabbab (effect) is hidden, we refer to the sabab (apparent cause) out of necessity.
4
Note the practical application
If a bloated dead animal is found, repeat all prayers from the past three days. If not bloated, repeat only those from the past day and night. This is based on the legal maxim that 'the apparent cause takes the ruling of the real cause when the real cause is hidden.'
Time less than a day is mere hours, too variable to fix; hence the one-day minimum.
5
Evaluate the legal reasoning
This ruling is a form of istihsan (juristic preference) based on necessity. Precise determination is impossible, so Sharia provides a practical estimate. The analogy with murder shows consistency in Hanafi legal theory.
The three-day limit also appears in other contexts, like praying at the grave of an unknown date.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الماء قد تحقق وهو سبب ظاهر للموت والموت فيه في نفس الأمر قد خفي، فيجب اعتباره مات فيه إحالة على السبب الظاهر عند خفاء المسبب دون الموهوم وهو الموت بسبب آخر كمن جرح إنساناً ولم يزل صاحب فراش فراش حتى مات يضاف موته إلى الجرح حتى يجب القصاص وإن احتمل موته بسبب آخر، وكذا إذا وجد قتيل في محله يضاف القتل إلى أهلها حتى تجب القسامة والدية عليهم وإن احتمل أنه قتل في موضع آخر غير أن الانتفاخ دليل التقادم فيقدر بالثلاث ولهذا يصلي على القبر إلى ثلاثة أيام على ما قيل، وعدم الانتفاخ دليل قرب العهد فقدرناه بيوم وليلة لأن ما دون ذلك ساعات لا يمكن ضبطها لتفاوتها.
In water, it has been realized that it is an apparent cause of death, while the actual death in itself is hidden. So it is obligatory to consider that he died in it, referring to the apparent cause when the effect is hidden, not the imagined one which is death by another cause. Like if someone wounds a person and the wounded person remains bedridden until he dies, his death is attributed to the wound, so retaliation becomes obligatory even if it is possible that he died by another cause. Similarly, if a slain person is found in a locality, the killing is attributed to its inhabitants, so qasama and blood money become obligatory on them, even if it is possible that he was killed elsewhere. However, bloating is evidence of oldness, so it is estimated as three days, and for this reason, prayer is performed at the grave up to three days according to what is said. The lack of bloating is evidence of recent occurrence, so we estimated it as a day and a night because what is less than that is hours that cannot be fixed due to their variability.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof that establishes the time limit for najasah in a well using the principle of attributing an effect to its apparent cause when the actual cause is hidden. The commentator uses the analogy of murder cases to justify the three-day and one-day limits. In Hanafi law, when a wounded person dies after being bedridden, the death is attributed to the wound even if another cause is possible, because the wound is the apparent cause. Similarly, when a dead body is found in a locality, the inhabitants are held responsible via qasama. The commentator applies this principle to the well: if a dead animal falls in and the time of its death is unknown, we look for signs. If the animal is bloated, it indicates that it fell in three days ago, so prayers of the past three days must be repeated. If not bloated, it indicates a recent fall within a day and night. This is based on the maxim that when the actual time is hidden, we refer to the apparent sign. The ruling is a form of istihsan based on necessity, as precise determination is impossible.

Key Terms
سبب ظاهر (sabab zahir): Apparent cause that is evident to the observer, as opposed to the hidden real cause (musabbab).
انتفاخ (intifakh): Bloating of a corpse; used as a sign of time elapsed (three days) for najasah in a well.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the time limit for repeating prayers when a dead animal is found bloated in a well?
A) One day and night
B) Three days
C) Only the current prayer
D) No repeat required

Key Takeaway
Hanafi jurists use the apparent cause principle, analogized from murder cases, to estimate the time of najasah in a well as three days if bloated, one day if not.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Time Limit for Najasah on Garment
Resolving the dispute between Abu Hanifa and his companions on the number of prayers to repeat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the number of past prayers to repeat when najasah is discovered on a garment, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Dry najasah: repeat three days; wet: one day. Sahibayn: only the current prayer. Which is mufta bihi?

A worshipper discovers najasah on his garment during prayer or after completing several prayers. He does not know when it occurred. How many past prayers must he repeat? This is a classic intra-Hanafi dispute where Abu Hanifa and his two major students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagree. Understanding their proofs and the tarjih mechanism is essential for correct practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that the person does not know when the najasah occurred. The garment is visible, so if it were old, the person would likely have noticed. The well is hidden, so different rules apply.
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa: repeat prayers of three days if dry, one day if wet. Sahibayn: only repeat the prayer in which awareness occurred or at most the immediate previous prayer.
The difference is in the time period, not the obligation to repeat.
3
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa analogizes to the well (hidden najasah, repeat three days) and adds physical signs: dry indicates age. Sahibayn rely on certainty: the garment is visible, so the najasah must be recent.
The Sahibayn's proof is based on the principle of certainty (yaqin).
4
Understand the tarjih
Most Hanafi books favor Abu Hanifa's view as a precaution (ihtiyat) in worship. Some later scholars like al-Itabi prefer the Sahibayn for ease.
The commentator says the majority act on Abu Hanifa's view.
5
Apply to a modern scenario
If a person finds a dry spot of najasah on his sleeve after praying the previous three days, according to mufta bihi he must repeat all prayers of those three days. If the spot is wet, he repeats only the past day and night.
This shows the practical difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما مسألة النجاسة فقد قال المعلى بن منصور الرازي تلميذهما إنها على الخلاف، فإن كانت يابسة يعيد صلاة ثلاثة أيام، وإن كانت طرية يعيد صلاة يوم وليلة عنده فلا يحتاج إلى الفرق، ولو سلم أنها على الوفاق كما قدمنا أنه الأصح فالفرق له واضح وهو أن الثوب بمرأى عينه يقع عليه بصره فلو كانت النجاسة أصابته قبل ذلك لعلم بها بخلاف البئر فإنها غائبة عن بصره القياس، وما ذكره المعلى رحمه الله يحتمل كونه رواية عن الإمام وهو ظاهر ما ذكره الاسبيجابي وصاحب البدائع، ويحتمل أنه تفقه منه بطريق القياس على مسألة البئر وهو ظاهر ما في المحيط وهو الحق فقد قال الحاكم الشهيد: إن المعلى قال ذلك من دأب نفسه. وأما مسألة الميراث فالمرأة محتاجة إلى الاستحقاق والظاهر لا يصلح حجة لها وإنما يصلح للدفع والورثة هم الدافعون وفي المجتبى وحكم ما عجن به حكم الوضوء والغسل. وكان الصباغي يفتي بقول أبي حنيفة فيما يتعلق بالصلاة وبقولهما فيما سواه. كذا في معراج الدراية. وفي غاية البيان وما قاله أبو حنيفة احتياط في أمر العبادة، وما قالاه عمل باليقين ورفق بالناس. وفي تصحيح الشيخ قاسم رحمه الله وفي فتاوى العتابي : المختار قولهما . قلت : هو المخالف لعامة الكتب فقد رجح دليله في كثير من الكتب وقالوا: إنه الاحتياط فكان العمل عليه. وذكر الاسبيجابي أن ما عجن به قال بعضهم يلقى إلى الكلاب، وقال بعضهم يعلف المواشي، وقال بعضهم يباع من شافعي المذهب أو داودي المذهب اهـ. واختار الأول في البدائع وجزم به بصيغة قال مشايخنا يطعم للكلاب.
As for the issue of najasah, Mu'alla ibn Mansur al-Razi, their student, said that it is based on disagreement: if it is dry, one repeats prayers of three days; if it is wet, one repeats prayers of a day and a night according to him, so there is no need for distinction. And if one concedes that it is based on agreement, as we mentioned that the more correct view, then the distinction is clear: the garment is in front of one's eyes and his vision falls upon it, so if najasah had affected it before, he would have known it, unlike the well which is hidden from his sight by analogy. What Mu'alla mentioned may be a narration from the Imam, and this is apparent from what al-Isbijabi and the author of al-Bada'i' mentioned, and it may be that he deduced it by way of analogy to the well issue, and this is apparent from what is in al-Muhit, and it is the truth, for al-Hakim al-Shahid said: Mu'alla said that from his own effort. As for the issue of inheritance, the woman needs to establish entitlement, and the apparent (presumption) is not a valid proof for her, but is valid for defense, and the heirs are the defenders. In al-Mujtaba: the ruling of what is kneaded with (najas water) is the same as the ruling of wudu and ghusl. Al-Sabbighi used to give fatwa according to Abu Hanifa's view regarding matters related to prayer, and according to their view in other matters. It is like that in Mi'raj al-Diraya. In Ghayat al-Bayan: what Abu Hanifa said is precautionary in matters of worship, and what they said is acting upon certainty and ease for people. In Tashih al-Shaykh Qasim and in Fatawa al-Itabi: the chosen view is their view. I (the commentator) say: this opposes the majority of books, for his (Abu Hanifa's) evidence has been favored in many books, and they said it is the precautionary measure, so one should act upon it. Al-Isbijabi mentioned that regarding what is kneaded with it, some said it should be thrown to dogs, some said feed it to livestock, some said sell it to a Shafi'i or Dawudi. The first is chosen in al-Bada'i' and stated definitively in the form: our masters said feed it to dogs.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute about the time limit for repeating prayers due to najasah on a garment. The core issue: when a person discovers najasah on his garment without knowing when it occurred, how many past prayers must he repeat? Mu'alla ibn Mansur reports that Abu Hanifa distinguishes: if the najasah is dry, three days' prayers are repeated; if wet, one day and night. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold that only the prayer in which the person became aware is repeated, plus perhaps the immediate previous one, based on certainty. The commentator notes that some later scholars preferred the Sahibayn's view for ease, but the majority of Hanafi texts (like al-Bada'i' and al-Muhit) consider Abu Hanifa's view as the precautionary measure (ihtiyat) in worship and thus the operative position. The passage also includes the ruling on dough kneaded with najas water: it should be fed to dogs, not wasted entirely, showing a pragmatic approach. The dispute is based on the principle of attribution to the apparent cause: the garment is directly visible, so if najasah were old, the person would have seen it; thus the najasah is considered recent. For a well, the najasah is hidden, so a longer period is assumed. Abu Hanifa's distinction between dry and wet is based on physical signs: dry indicates age, wet indicates freshness. The Sahibayn rely on the principle of certainty (yaqin) – only the prayer in which the najasah became known is definitely invalid; others are presumed valid unless proven. The commentator concludes that despite some later preference for the Sahibayn, the majority of Hanafi scholarship acts on Abu Hanifa's view due to its being more precautionary in worship.

Key Terms
نجاسة (najasah): Impurity that prevents the validity of prayer if present on the body, garment, or place of prayer.
ثوب (thawb): Garment; the item of clothing on which najasah is found.
احتياط (ihtiyat): Precautionary measure in worship; adopting the stricter view to ensure validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi texts, which view is mufta bihi regarding repetition of prayers due to najasah on a garment?
A) Sahibayn's view: only the current prayer
B) Abu Hanifa's view: three days if dry, one day if wet
C) The view of al-Itabi: no repetition needed
D) The view of the Shafi'is: repeat all prayers

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the mufta bihi view for najasah on a garment is Abu Hanifa's: repeat three days if dry, one day if wet, based on ihtiyat.

Ruling on Dough Kneaded with Najas Water
Ruling on disposing of dough kneaded with impure water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dough made with water containing najasah and identify the recommended method of disposal.

Core Idea
Dough kneaded with najas water should be fed to dogs to avoid waste, not consumed by humans.

Sometimes a person unknowingly uses water that has become najis (impure) to knead dough. What should be done with that dough? The Hanafi school offers a pragmatic solution: it should be fed to dogs or other permissible uses, rather than being discarded entirely. This ruling balances ritual purity with economic practicality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Dough is made with water that is known to be najis (e.g., a dog drank from it, or an impure substance fell into it). The dough itself is now Najis because the water is Najis.
The water is considered mutanajjis (impure), not the flour.
2
State the legal classification
The dough is Najis and cannot be consumed by humans. It is not recommended to throw it away because that wastes property.
Waste is discouraged in Islam.
3
Present the options mentioned
Scholars gave several options: feed it to dogs (the preferred), feed it to livestock, or sell it to a non-Hanafi (Shafi'i or Dawudi) who considers it pure.
Each option has its own ruling regarding permissibility.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The author of al-Bada'i' chooses the first option: feed it to dogs. This is stated definitively in the form 'our masters said feed it to dogs'.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما مسألة النجاسة فقد قال المعلى بن منصور الرازي تلميذهما إنها على الخلاف، فإن كانت يابسة يعيد صلاة ثلاثة أيام، وإن كانت طرية يعيد صلاة يوم وليلة عنده فلا يحتاج إلى الفرق، ولو سلم أنها على الوفاق كما قدمنا أنه الأصح فالفرق له واضح وهو أن الثوب بمرأى عينه يقع عليه بصره فلو كانت النجاسة أصابته قبل ذلك لعلم بها بخلاف البئر فإنها غائبة عن بصره القياس، وما ذكره المعلى رحمه الله يحتمل كونه رواية عن الإمام وهو ظاهر ما ذكره الاسبيجابي وصاحب البدائع، ويحتمل أنه تفقه منه بطريق القياس على مسألة البئر وهو ظاهر ما في المحيط وهو الحق فقد قال الحاكم الشهيد: إن المعلى قال ذلك من دأب نفسه. وأما مسألة الميراث فالمرأة محتاجة إلى الاستحقاق والظاهر لا يصلح حجة لها وإنما يصلح للدفع والورثة هم الدافعون وفي المجتبى وحكم ما عجن به حكم الوضوء والغسل. وكان الصباغي يفتي بقول أبي حنيفة فيما يتعلق بالصلاة وبقولهما فيما سواه. كذا في معراج الدراية. وفي غاية البيان وما قاله أبو حنيفة احتياط في أمر العبادة، وما قالاه عمل باليقين ورفق بالناس. وفي تصحيح الشيخ قاسم رحمه الله وفي فتاوى العتابي : المختار قولهما . قلت : هو المخالف لعامة الكتب فقد رجح دليله في كثير من الكتب وقالوا: إنه الاحتياط فكان العمل عليه. وذكر الاسبيجابي أن ما عجن به قال بعضهم يلقى إلى الكلاب، وقال بعضهم يعلف المواشي، وقال بعضهم يباع من شافعي المذهب أو داودي المذهب اهـ. واختار الأول في البدائع وجزم به بصيغة قال مشايخنا يطعم للكلاب.
As for the issue of najasah, Mu'alla ibn Mansur al-Razi, their student, said that it is based on disagreement: if it is dry, one repeats prayers of three days; if it is wet, one repeats prayers of a day and a night according to him, so there is no need for distinction. And if one concedes that it is based on agreement, as we mentioned that the more correct view, then the distinction is clear: the garment is in front of one's eyes and his vision falls upon it, so if najasah had affected it before, he would have known it, unlike the well which is hidden from his sight by analogy. What Mu'alla mentioned may be a narration from the Imam, and this is apparent from what al-Isbijabi and the author of al-Bada'i' mentioned, and it may be that he deduced it by way of analogy to the well issue, and this is apparent from what is in al-Muhit, and it is the truth, for al-Hakim al-Shahid said: Mu'alla said that from his own effort. As for the issue of inheritance, the woman needs to establish entitlement, and the apparent (presumption) is not a valid proof for her, but is valid for defense, and the heirs are the defenders. In al-Mujtaba: the ruling of what is kneaded with (najas water) is the same as the ruling of wudu and ghusl. Al-Sabbighi used to give fatwa according to Abu Hanifa's view regarding matters related to prayer, and according to their view in other matters. It is like that in Mi'raj al-Diraya. In Ghayat al-Bayan: what Abu Hanifa said is precautionary in matters of worship, and what they said is acting upon certainty and ease for people. In Tashih al-Shaykh Qasim and in Fatawa al-Itabi: the chosen view is their view. I (the commentator) say: this opposes the majority of books, for his (Abu Hanifa's) evidence has been favored in many books, and they said it is the precautionary measure, so one should act upon it. Al-Isbijabi mentioned that regarding what is kneaded with it, some said it should be thrown to dogs, some said feed it to livestock, some said sell it to a Shafi'i or Dawudi. The first is chosen in al-Bada'i' and stated definitively in the form: our masters said feed it to dogs.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute about the time limit for repeating prayers due to najasah on a garment. The core issue: when a person discovers najasah on his garment without knowing when it occurred, how many past prayers must he repeat? Mu'alla ibn Mansur reports that Abu Hanifa distinguishes: if the najasah is dry, three days' prayers are repeated; if wet, one day and night. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold that only the prayer in which the person became aware is repeated, plus perhaps the immediate previous one, based on certainty. The commentator notes that some later scholars preferred the Sahibayn's view for ease, but the majority of Hanafi texts (like al-Bada'i' and al-Muhit) consider Abu Hanifa's view as the precautionary measure (ihtiyat) in worship and thus the operative position. The passage also includes the ruling on dough kneaded with najas water: it should be fed to dogs, not wasted entirely, showing a pragmatic approach. The dispute is based on the principle of attribution to the apparent cause: the garment is directly visible, so if najasah were old, the person would have seen it; thus the najasah is considered recent. For a well, the najasah is hidden, so a longer period is assumed. Abu Hanifa's distinction between dry and wet is based on physical signs: dry indicates age, wet indicates freshness. The Sahibayn rely on the principle of certainty (yaqin) – only the prayer in which the najasah became known is definitely invalid; others are presumed valid unless proven. The commentator concludes that despite some later preference for the Sahibayn, the majority of Hanafi scholarship acts on Abu Hanifa's view due to its being more precautionary in worship.

Key Terms
عجين (ajin): Dough, typically made from flour and water.
ماء نجس (ma' najis): Water that has become impure due to contact with najasah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the preferred method of disposing of dough kneaded with najis water?
A) Throw it in the trash
B) Feed it to dogs
C) Feed it to livestock
D) Sell it to a non-Muslim

Key Takeaway
The preferred Hanafi ruling for dough kneaded with najis water is to feed it to dogs, balancing purity with the prohibition of waste.

Finding Maniyy and Blood on Clothing: Tracing the Time
Rulings on maniyy vs blood on clothing when time unknown

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for maniyy and blood on a garment when the time is unknown, and apply the principle of tracing from the last cause of emission.

Core Idea
Maniyy requires repeating prayers from the last cause; blood does not because it may be from another.

Imagine you wake up and find a dried stain on your garment. You don't know when it occurred. Is it semen or blood? The Hanafi school distinguishes clearly: semen obligates repeating prayers from the last wet dream or intercourse, while blood does not require any repetition. This lesson explains the reasoning and the general rule for other impurities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of najasah
Determine whether the stain is maniyy (semen) or blood. The ruling depends on this identification.
Blood is less definitive because it may belong to another person.
2
Apply the ruling for maniyy
If it is maniyy and the time is unknown, repeat all prayers from the last time you had a wet dream (ihtilam) or intercourse.
The cause of emission anchors the obligation.
3
Apply the ruling for blood
If it is blood, no repetition is required because the blood may have come from someone else touching the garment.
The default is that it is not necessarily your own blood.
4
Generalize to other najasat
The same principle applies to urine (last urination) and nosebleed (last ru'af). For semen, it is the last wet dream or intercourse.
This is the position of the Mashayikh.
5
Note the exception for shared garments
If the garment is worn by multiple people, both blood and semen are treated the same (both require repetition) because the source is uncertain.
This is from al-Bada'i'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فروع: ذكر ابن رستم في نوادره عن أبي حنيفة من وجد في ثوبه منياً أعاد من آخر ما احتلم، وإن كان دماً لا يعيد لأن دم غيره قد يصيبه والظاهر أن الإصابة لم تتقدم زمان يصيب ثوبه فالظاهر أنه منيه فيعتبر وجوده من وقت وجود سبب خروجه حتى إن الثوب لو كان مما يلبسه هو وغيره يستوي فيه حكم الدم والمني. ومشايخنا قالوا في البول يعتبر من آخر ما بال وفي الدم من آخر ما رعف، وفي المني من آخر ما احتلم أو جامع كذا في البدائع. ومراده بالاحتلام النوم لأنه سببه بدليل ما نقله في المحيط عن ابن رستم أنه يعيد من آخر نومه نامها فيه واختار في المحيط أنه لا يعيد شيئاً لو رأى دماً. ولو فتق جبة فوجد فيها فأرة ميتة ولم يعلم متى دخل فيها فإن لم يكن للجبة ثقب يعيد ندف القطن فيها، وإن كان فيه ثقب يعيد صلاة ثلاثة أيام ولياليها عند أبي حنيفة كما في البئر. كذا في التنجيس والمحيط. وفي الذخيرة ولا بأس برش الماء النجس في الطريق ولا يسقى للبهائم. وفي خزانة الفتاوى: لا بأس بأن يسقى الماء النجس للبقر والإبل والغنم وحيث وجبت الإعادة على قوله فالمعاد الصلوات الخمس والوتر وسنة الفجر. كذا في شرح منية المصلي.
Branches: Ibn Rustam mentioned in his Nawadir from Abu Hanifa: whoever finds semen on his garment repeats from the last time he had a wet dream; if it is blood, he does not repeat because the blood of someone else may have affected him, and the apparent (presumption) is that the affliction did not precede the time when it could affect his garment, so the apparent is that it is his semen, so its existence is considered from the time of the cause of its emergence. Even if the garment is worn by him and others, the ruling of blood and semen is the same. Our masters said about urine: it is considered from the last time he urinated; about blood: from the last time he had a nosebleed; about semen: from the last time he had a wet dream or had intercourse. This is in al-Bada'i'. He meant by wet dream: sleep, because it is the cause, as evidenced by what al-Muhit narrated from Ibn Rustam: he repeats from the last sleep he slept in it. And al-Muhit chose that he does not repeat anything if he sees blood. If he rips open a cloak and finds a dead mouse in it, not knowing when it entered: if the cloak has no hole, he repeats the entire sprinkling of cotton (i.e., prayer covering that day); if it has a hole, he repeats prayers of three days and their nights according to Abu Hanifa, as in the well. This is in al-Tanjis and al-Muhit. In al-Dhakhira: there is no harm in sprinkling najas water on the road, and it should not be given to animals to drink. In Khizanat al-Fatawa: there is no harm in giving najas water to cows, camels, and sheep. And wherever repetition becomes obligatory according to his view, what is repeated are the five prayers, witr, and the sunnah of Fajr. This is in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents several furu' (subsidiary rulings) from Ibn Rustam's Nawadir based on Abu Hanifa's teachings. The first ruling: if a person finds semen (maniyy) on his garment and does not know when it occurred, he must repeat prayers from the last time he had a wet dream or intercourse. This is because the semen is presumed to be his own and the latest possible occurrence is that time. For blood, however, no repetition is required because the blood could belong to someone else (e.g., a wound from another person) that may have touched the garment without the owner knowing; thus, the appearance of blood is not necessarily linked to his own body. The passage also distinguishes between different fluids: urine is traced to the last urination, blood to the last nosebleed, semen to the last wet dream or intercourse. A later narration from al-Muhit says that if one sees blood but doubts, no repeat at all. Another branch: if a cloak (jubbah) is ripped open and a dead mouse is found inside, the repetition depends on whether the cloak had a hole. If no hole, the mouse must have entered when the cloak was being made, so only that day's prayers are repeated (the time of construction). If there is a hole, the mouse could have entered anytime, so three days' prayers are repeated (like the well ruling). Additional rulings: sprinkling najas water on the road is permissible; but feeding najas water to animals is generally discouraged, though later fatwa allows it for livestock. Finally, when repetition is required, it includes the five daily prayers, witr, and the sunnah of Fajr.

Key Terms
احتلام (ihtilam): Wet dream; the emission of semen during sleep. Used as the cause for tracing the time of maniyy on a garment.
رعاف (ru'af): Nosebleed; a cause of blood emission traced for blood on a garment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for finding blood on a garment when the time is unknown?
A) Repeat prayers from the last nosebleed.
B) Repeat prayers from the last three days.
C) No repetition is required because it could be someone else's blood.
D) Repeat only the current prayer.

Key Takeaway
Maniyy requires tracing to the last cause; blood does not, unless the garment is shared.

Dead Mouse in a Cloak and Related Najasah Rulings
Rulings on dead mouse in cloak and najas water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the number of prayers to repeat when a dead mouse is found in a cloak, and state the rulings on sprinkling and feeding najas water.

Core Idea
The presence of a hole determines whether to repeat one day or three days of prayers for a hidden dead mouse.

Sometimes impurity is discovered inside clothing after prayer. How many prayers must be repeated? The answer depends on whether the clothing had a hole that could allow entry. This lesson also covers rulings on using najas water on roads and feeding it to animals, as well as which prayers are repeated when repetition is wajib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario: dead mouse found in a cloak
A cloak (jubbah) is ripped open and a dead mouse is found inside. The time of entry is unknown.
This is a specific case mentioned by Abu Hanifa.
2
Check condition: does the cloak have a hole?
If the cloak has no hole, the mouse must have entered during construction, so only that day's prayers are repeated (the cotton-plucking). If it has a hole, the mouse could have entered anytime, so three days of prayers are repeated.
The analogy is to the well: if a dead animal is found in a well, three days of prayers are repeated.
3
Apply the ruling for najas water
Sprinkling najas water on the road is permissible. However, it should not be given to animals to drink, but later fatwa allowed it for cows, camels, and sheep.
This shows flexibility in later Hanafi practice.
4
Determine which prayers are repeated
When repetition is obligatory, it includes the five daily prayers, witr, and the sunnah of Fajr.
Other sunnah prayers are not included.

The Ruling on Sweat and Su'r of Animals: The Donkey Case
Expansion on the principle that sweat follows su'r with evidence for the donkey

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sweat and su'r of a donkey and explain the evidence and intra-madhhab difference.

Core Idea
The sweat of an animal follows the ruling of its su'r; the donkey's sweat is pure due to the Prophet riding it bareback.

A key principle in Hanafi taharah is that an animal's sweat (araq) takes the same ruling as its su'r (the water it leaves behind). This is because both are bodily fluids originating from the animal's flesh. While this principle is straightforward for most animals, the donkey presents a special case due to a conflict between the general rule (animals with inedible meat have impure su'r) and the specific honor shown by the Prophet ﷺ. This lesson unpacks the evidence and clarifies the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The passage states: 'wa al-araq ka al-su'r' (sweat is like su'r). Understand that the ruling of any animal's sweat is deduced from its su'r, which itself is linked to the permissibility of eating its meat.
Su'r is the leftover water after drinking.
2
Define su'r and its legal basis
Su'r of an animal is mixed with saliva and, like sweat, dissolves from the flesh. Therefore, its ruling (purity vs. impurity) stems from the ruling of the animal's meat (edible vs. non-edible carnivore).
Edible animal → su'r pure; carnivore → su'r impure; donkey is neither.
3
Apply to the special case of the donkey
A donkey's meat is not eaten, and it is not a carnivore. According to the principle, its su'r would be doubtful. However, the passage argues that the donkey's sweat is pure, citing a unique evidence.
The doubt is not about purity, but about tahuriyyah (ability to purify).
4
Trace the evidence: the Prophet's riding
The commentator explains that the Prophet ﷺ rode a donkey bareback (ma'ruriyyan). This honor indicates that the donkey's sweat must be pure, as it inevitably touched the Prophet's body.
The grammatical point about 'ma'ruriyyan' reinforces that the situation required the sweat to be pure.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and mufta bihi
Some Hanafis doubted the purity of donkey's su'r, but the more correct (asahh) view is that it is pure (tahir), though a minority held it could not be used for wudu (tahuriyyah). The mufta bihi is that both sweat and su'r are pure.
The passage mentions Qadikhan's view that the doubt is merely about tahuriyyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعرق كالسؤر لما فرغ من بيان فساد الماء وعدمه باعتبار وقوع نفس الحيوانات فيه ذكرهما باعتبار ما يتولد منها . والسؤر مهموز العين بقية الماء التي يبقيها الشارب في الإناء أو في الحوض ثم استعير لبقية الطعام وغيره، والجمع الاسآر والفعل أسأر أي أبقى مما شرب أي عرق كل شيء معتبر بسوره طهارة ونجاسة وكراهة لأن السؤر مختلط باللعاب وهو والعرق متولدان من اللحم إذ كل واحد منهما رطوبة متحللة من اللحم فأخذا حكمه. ولا ينتقض بعرق الحمار فإنه طاهر مع أن سؤره مشكوك فيه لأنا نقول : خص بركوبه صلى الله عليه وسلم الحمار معرورياً والحر حر الحجاز والثقل ثقل النبوة فلا بد أن يعرق الحمار. قال في المغرب: فرس عري لا سرج عليه ولا لبد وجمعه أعراء، ولا يقال فرس عريان كما لا يقال رجل عرى، واعرورى الدابة ركبه عرياً ومنه كان عليه السلام يركب الحمار معرورياً وهو حال من ضمير الفاعل المستكن ولو كان من المفعول لقيل معرورى أهـ. أو لأنه لا فرق بين عرقه وسؤره فإن سؤره طاهر على الأصح والشك إنما هو في طهوريته، وقد ذكر قاضيخان في شرح الجامع مع أن سؤره مشكوك فيه أي مشكوك في طهارته، وهذا بناء على قول البعض وهو غير الأصح كما سيأتي . ثم هنا بحث وهو أنه إن كان المراد بطهارة عرق الحمار طهارته في نفسه كما يقتضيه الجواب الأول لزم أنه لو وقع في ماء لا يصيره مشكوكاً في طهارته ولا في طهوريته لأن ما وقع فيه على هذا طاهر لا شك فيه وهو مخالف لما سيأتي، وإن كان المراد طهارة الماء الذي أصابه كما يقتضيه الجواب الثاني الآتي لم يصلح الجواب الأول للجوابية تأمل. قوله : (قال في المغرب فرس عرى الخ) الاولى الإتيان بـ لكن ليفيد الاستدراك على ما قبله كما فعل في النهر فإن مبنى الاستدلال على طهارته على أن معرورياً حال من الحمار، وأما على ما في المغرب من أنه حال من ضمير الفاعل فلا دلالة لكن في كونه حالاً من الفاعلى بعد لا يخفى إذ يبعد من حاله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يركب وهو عريان. وقد
And sweat is like su'r. After finishing the explanation of the corruption of water or lack thereof due to animals falling into it, he mentioned these two with regard to what is produced from them. Su'r (with a hamza on the 'ayn) is the remaining water that the drinker leaves in the vessel or basin, then borrowed for leftover food etc. The plural is as'ar, and the verb is as'ara: he left over from what he drank. So the sweat of everything is considered according to its su'r in purity, impurity, and karahah, because the su'r is mixed with saliva, and both (su'r and sweat) are generated from flesh, as each of them is a moisture that dissolves from flesh, so they take its ruling. It is not invalidated by the sweat of a donkey, for it is pure even though its su'r is doubtful, because we say: the donkey was honored by the Prophet (peace be upon him) riding it bareback, and 'urri is a barren land of Hijaz, and the load is the load of prophethood, so the donkey must sweat. He said in al-Mughrib: 'A horse that is bare: no saddle on it and no felt, its plural is a'ra'. It is not said 'frass 'aryan' just as it is not said 'rajul 'ari'. I'tarada al-dabbata: he rode it bareback. Among these is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to ride the donkey bareback, and it is a state (hal) from the hidden pronoun of the subject. If it were from the object, it would have been 'ma'ruriyyan'. End quote. Or because there is no difference between its sweat and its su'r, for its su'r is pure according to the most correct view, and the doubt is only regarding its tahuriyyah (capacity to purify). Qadikhan mentioned in Sharh al-Jami': 'even though its su'r is doubtful' meaning doubt in its purity. This is based on the view of some, which is not the most correct, as will come. Then there is a discussion here: if what is meant by the purity of donkey's sweat is its purity in itself, as the first answer implies, then it would follow that if it falls into water, it would not make it doubtful in its purity nor in its tahuriyyah, because what falls into it in this case is pure without doubt, and this contradicts what will come. If what is meant is the purity of the water that it touches, as the second answer implies, then the first answer is not suitable for being an answer. Consider. He said: He said in al-Mughrib: 'a bare horse...' The better phrasing would be to use 'but' (lakin) to indicate an exception, as done in al-Nahr, because the basis of the inference for its purity is that 'ma'ruriyyan' is a state of the donkey. As for what is in al-Mughrib, that it is a state of the pronoun of the subject, there is no indication. However, that it is a state of the subject is not hidden, for it is far from the state of the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he would ride while bare. And he has...
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage defines the legal status of sweat ('araq) and su'r (remnant water/liquid) of animals, establishing a principle: the sweat of an animal has the same ruling as its su'r (the water left after it drinks). The su'r is mixed with saliva, and both are bodily fluids; they derive their ruling from the animal's meat (i.e., whether the meat is edible or not). For example, the sweat of a horse (whose meat is edible) is pure; the sweat of a camel is pure, etc. The discussion then focuses on the donkey, a problematic case. A donkey is not a carnivore, but its meat is not eaten. Some Hanafi texts doubt the purity of its su'r, but the sweat of the donkey is considered pure. The commentator explains that the donkey was honored by the Prophet riding it bareback, which indicates its sweat must be pure. The debate is about whether the donkey's su'r is pure or merely capable of purification (tahuriyyah). The final view is that the su'r of a donkey is pure (tahir) but not purifying (tahir) for other things? Actually, the text discusses whether its sweat, if falling into water, renders the water doubtful or not. The commentator notes that the correct view is that the donkey's su'r is pure, but the doubt is about its capacity to make other things pure (tahuriyyah). The passage ends with a grammatical point about the word 'ma'ruriyyan' to support the argument that the Prophet rode the donkey bareback, which strengthens the purity ruling.

Key Terms
سؤر (su'r): The leftover water or liquid after an animal drinks; its ruling determines the ruling of its sweat and saliva.
عرق (araq): Sweat of an animal; its ruling is analogous to its su'r because both are bodily fluids.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for considering the sweat of a donkey pure?
A) The donkey's meat is edible
B) The donkey is a carnivore
C) The Prophet rode it bareback
D) All animals' sweat is pure

Key Takeaway
The sweat of a donkey is pure (tahir) because the Prophet rode it bareback, and the general principle that sweat follows su'r applies.

The Ruling on Donkey Sweat and Saliva: Three Narrations and the Relied-Upon View
Three narrations on the ruling of donkey/mule sweat and saliva

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi narrations on donkey sweat/saliva and identify the mufta bihi ruling on its effect on garments and water.

Core Idea
Donkey sweat is pure in the Hanafi school, but if it falls into water, the water becomes problematic for purification.

The sweat and saliva of donkeys and mules have been subject to multiple narrations within the Hanafi school. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents three positions, reconciles them, and clarifies the relied-upon view. Understanding this dispute helps you grasp the Hanafi methodology of weighing narrations and the concept of mā' mushkil (problematic water).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three narrations
The passage begins by listing three rulings on the effect of donkey saliva/sweat when it touches a garment or body: measured by a dirham, by excessive amount, and no effect at all. Note which one is relied upon.
The third narration (no effect) is the mufta bihi.
2
Examine the intermediate positions
Consider the views of Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani (impure but excused for necessity) and al-Walwalji (pure for body, spoils water). Understand how they differ from the relied-upon view.
Al-Walwalji's distinction between body and water becomes key.
3
Analyze the author's critique
The author questions how something pure can spoil water's purifying quality. He then prefers Qadikhan's interpretation that excusal only applies to body/garment, not to water.
This reveals the internal logic of ranking proofs within the school.
4
Identify the final mufta bihi
Synthesize the author's conclusion: sweat and saliva are pure, they do not soil the body/garment, but they render water problematic (mā' mushkil) if they fall into it.
Problematic water is pure but not fit for wudu/ghusl.
5
Recognize the error in al-Atqani's claim
The passage rejects al-Atqani's claim of consensus on absolute purity, showing that the settled view is not absolute but limited by the 'problematic water' exception.
This demonstrates the importance of precise wording in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة الصغير ثلاث روايات في لعابه وعرقه إذا أصاب الثوب أو البدن في رواية مقدر بالدرهم، وفي رواية بالكثير الفاحش، وفي رواية لا يمنع وإن فحش وعليه الاعتماد، وذكر شمس الأئمة الحلواني أن عرقه نجس لكن عفي عنه للضرورة. فعلى هذا لو وقع في الماء القليل يفسده وهكذا روي عن أبي يوسف أهـ. وذكر الولوالجي رحمه الله أن عرق الحمار والبغل إذا أصاب الثوب لا يفسده، ولو وقع في الماء أفسده يعني به لم يبق طهوراً لأن عرقهما إذا وقع في الماء صار الماء مشكلاً كما في لعابهما، والماء المشكل طاهر لكن كونه طهوراً مشكل فلا يزول الحدث الثابت بيقين بالشك .اهـ. وهكذا في التنجيس واعلم أن تفسير الفساد بعدم الطهورية فيه نظر لأنه إذا كان كل من العرق واللعاب طاهراً كيف يخرج الماء به عن الطهورية مع أنه فرض قليل والماء غالب عليه، فلعل الأشبه ما ذكره قاضيخان في تفسير قوله شمس الأئمة إنه نجس وعفي عنه في الثوب والبدن للضرورة في الماء كما لا يخفى. فالحاصل أنه لا فرق بين العرق والسؤر على ما هو المعتمد من أن كلاً منهما طاهر، وإذا أصاب الثوب أو البدن لا ينجسه، وإذا وقع في الماء صار مشكلاً، ولهذا قال في المستصفى: ظاهر المذهب أن العرق واللعاب مشكوك فيهما أهـ. فظهر بهذا كله أن قولهم إن العرق كالسؤر على إطلاقه من غير استثناء، وظهر به أيضاً أن ما نقله الأتقاني في شرح البزدوي من الإجماع على طهارة عرقه فليس مما ينبغي وكأنه بناه على أنها هي التي استقر عليها الحال.
The book of purification. There are three narrations regarding its saliva and sweat when it touches the garment or body: in one narration it is measured by a dirham, in another by excessive abundance, and in a third it does not prevent even if excessive, and this one is relied upon. Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani mentioned that its sweat is impure but excused due to necessity. According to this, if it falls into a small quantity of water, it spoils it, and similarly it is narrated from Abu Yusuf. Al-Walwalji (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned that the sweat of a donkey and mule, if it touches a garment, does not spoil it, but if it falls into water, it spoils it, meaning it does not remain purifying because when their sweat falls into water, the water becomes problematic, as with their saliva. Problematic water is pure but its purifying quality is problematic, so a certainty of impurity is not removed by doubt. Similarly in al-Tanjis. Know that interpreting 'spoil' as 'not being purifying' is questionable because if both sweat and saliva are pure, how can they remove water from its purifying state when the water is little and the water predominates? Perhaps the more appropriate is what Qadikhan mentioned in interpreting the statement of Shams al-A'immah that it is impure but excused in garment and body due to necessity, but not in water. The conclusion is that there is no difference between sweat and saliva according to the relied-upon view that each of them is pure; if it touches the garment or body it does not make it impure, and if it falls into water it becomes problematic. For this reason it is said in al-Mustasfa: 'The apparent of the madhhab is that sweat and saliva are doubted.' From all this it becomes clear that their statement 'sweat is like saliva' is general without exception, and also that what al-Atqani narrated in Sharh al-Bazdawi about consensus on its purity is not correct; it appears he built it on the view that settled upon.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the ruling of the sweat and saliva of donkeys and mules. The author presents three narrations regarding their effect when they contact the body or garment: one measures impurity by a dirham's area, another by excessive amount, and a third says they do not prevent prayer at all. The relied-upon view is the third: the sweat and saliva are pure and do not require washing for prayer. However, Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani considered the sweat impure but excused due to necessity. Al-Walwalji distinguishes between contact with the body (no impurity) and falling into water (makes water problematic, i.e., not valid for ablution). The author then critiques al-Halwani's view, saying that if both are pure, how can they spoil water? He prefers Qadikhan's interpretation that the excusal is only for the body and garment, not for water. The final conclusion is that the correct position is that sweat and saliva are pure; they do not soil the body/garment, but make water problematic (so it can remove impurity but not be used for ritual purification due to doubt). The passage ends by criticizing al-Atqani's claim of consensus on absolute purity.

Key Terms
لعاب (lu'āb): Saliva, specifically of the donkey and mule in this passage.
عرق (‘araq): Sweat of the donkey and mule.
ماء مشكل (mā' mushkil): Water that is pure in essence but its purifying quality is doubtful due to contact with a pure substance that is suspected to be impure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It makes the garment impure and requires washing.
B) It does not make the garment impure at all.
C) It makes the garment impure only if the sweat is excessive.
D) It makes the garment impure but is excused under necessity.

Key Takeaway
Donkey and mule sweat/saliva are pure, but they render a small quantity of water problematic (mā' mushkil) for ritual purification.

The Remnant of Humans: Pure in All States
Ruling on human su'r with exceptions and evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on human su'r, identify the exception for a wine drinker, and explain why the dislike for drinking does not affect purification.

Core Idea
Human su'r is pure and purifying; the dislike for drinking is due to pleasure, not impurity.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on a human's leftover drink (su'r). While some might think that a person's state (janabah, menstruation) affects purity, the Hanafi school firmly holds that human su'r is pure and can be used for purification. However, there are nuances regarding drinking from a woman's remnant and the immediate su'r of a wine drinker.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling on human su'r
The matn states human su'r is pure. The commentator explains that saliva comes from pure flesh, and no distinction is made based on the person's state.
2
Notice the exception for a wine drinker
If a wine drinker drinks immediately, his su'r is impure due to the residue in his mouth. After swallowing three times, it becomes pure.
The impurity is in the mouth, not the person.
3
Distinguish between dislike for drinking and for purification
Some texts mention dislike for a man drinking from a woman's remnant. The commentator clarifies this applies only to drinking, not to wudu, and only for non-mahrams.
The dislike is based on potential pleasure, not impurity.
4
Analyze the hadith evidence
Two hadith are cited: the Prophet drinking from a cup after a Bedouin and after 'A'isha while menstruating, proving human su'r is pure.
Also the hadith 'The believer is not impure.'

His statement: (And the remnant of the human, the horse, and what is eaten for its meat is pure). As for the human, because his saliva is generated from pure flesh and it is only uneaten due to his honor, and there is no difference between the junub and the pure, the menstruating woman, the postpartum woman, the child and the adult, the Muslim and the disbeliever, the male and the female. Thus mentioned al-Zayla'i (may Allah have mercy on him). Meaning that all are pure and purifying without dislike, but there is consideration. For it is stated in al-Mujtaba, in the chapter on prohibition and permission, that the remnant of a woman is disliked for a man and his remnant for her; for this reason male and female were not mentioned in many books. But it may be said: The mentioned dislike is only in drinking, not in purification. And they excepted from this generality the remnant of a wine drinker if he drinks immediately; his remnant is impure not because of his flesh but because of the impurity of his mouth, as if he bled from his mouth. But if he waits enough time to wash his mouth with his saliva then drinks, it does not become impure. Thus in many books. It is said: The meaning is that he mounted while being bareback on the donkey; it is a participle from 'i'rawara' transitive, its object omitted due to being known. His statement: (And for this reason it is said in al-Mustasfa...) its apparent is that the doubt is in the sweat and saliva themselves, so the doubt is in their purity, as there is no purifying quality except if it is taken to mean the water that the sweat or saliva touched, i.e., its purifying quality is doubted. Ponder. His statement: (That the remnant of the woman is disliked for the man and his remnant for her) al-Ramli said: I say it must be restricted to other than the wife and mahrams, and the hadith of 'A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her) will come explicitly regarding the first. His statement: (It is only in drinking, not in purification) meaning not due to lack of purity but due to the pleasure the drinker obtains from the trace of the other. His statement: (But if he waits enough to wash his mouth with his saliva etc.) al-Nahr said: ...even if... And in al-Khulasa and al-Tanjis: A man drank wine; if there remains in his mouth spittle such that if that wine were on a garment the spittle would purify it, then his mouth becomes purified. This is the correct according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and the requirement of pouring is waived according to Abu Yusuf due to necessity. Its analogy: If an impurity touches his limb and he licks it until no trace remains, or a child vomits on his mother's breast and she sucks it until the trace disappears, it becomes pure, contrary to Muhammad in all these based on the impermissibility of removing impurity with other than absolute water, as will come. In some commentaries of al-Quduri: If the beard of the drinker is long, it makes the water impure even if he drinks after hours because the long hair, having become impure, is not purified by the tongue. It seems because the tongue cannot encompass all of it by moistening it with his saliva and then taking the wetness of the impurity repeatedly, otherwise it is not less than the lips and mouth in purification by saliva, as a branching from the statement of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the permissibility of removing impurity with other than water. Thus in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli. If it is said: The remnant of a junub should become impure according to the view that used water is impure because the duty is dropped by it, we say: What contacts the water from his mouth is drunk; we concede it is not drunk, but it is for need so it is not used by him, like inserting his hand into the jar to take out his cup, as we have mentioned in the chapter on waters. They have transmitted two narrations regarding the removal of hadath by this drinking; the apparent of their speech is a preference that it is removive, so the water does not become used due to hardship, but Ya'qub Pasha stated clearly that the correct is that the duty does not fall by it. And evidence for the purity of the human's remnant absolutely is what Malik narrated through al-Zuhri from Anas that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was brought milk mixed with water, and on his right was a Bedouin and on his left was Abu Bakr; he drank then gave it to the Bedouin and said: 'The rightmost, then the rightmost.' And Muslim and others narrated from 'A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her) she said: 'I used to drink while I was menstruating, and I would hand it to the Prophet (peace be upon him) and he would place his mouth on the place of mine.' And when the Prophet (peace be upon him) lodged some polytheists in the mosque and allowed them to stay overnight, as in the Sahihs, it is known that the intention of His statement 'Indeed the polytheists are impure' (9:28) is impurity in their beliefs. And it is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) met Hudhayfah and extended his hand to shake hands; Hudhayfah withdrew his hand and said: 'I am junub,' so he said: 'The believer is not impure.' Mentioned by al-Baghawi in al-Masabih. As for the remnant of the horse, there are two narrations from Abu Hanifa: the apparent narration is that it is purifying without dislike, and this is the statement of the two companions because the dislike of its meat according to him is due to respect because it is an instrument of jihad, not due to its impurity, so it does not affect the dislike of its remnant, and this is the correct. Thus in al-Bada'i' and others. As for the remnant of what is eaten for its meat, because he drank after drinking wine immediately, his remnant is impure unless he swallows his saliva three times according to the Imam. It is said: The second (narrator?) and the condition of pouring is waived in this case, and the restriction to three is applied by many. His statement: (But Ya'qub Pasha stated clearly that the correct is that the duty does not fall by it) al-Nahr said and the first is more appropriate. His statement: (As for the remnant of the horse) al-Nahr said: He specified it although it is included in what is eaten for its meat due to the difference in the cause of dislike, even if it is apparently...
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement about the su'r (remnant) of humans, horses, and halal animals being pure. The author first explains that human su'r is pure because it originates from pure flesh; no distinction is made between various states of the person (junub, menstruating, etc.) or between men and women. However, the author notes a disagreement: some sources dislike the su'r of a woman for a man (and vice versa) but this is restricted to drinking, not purification. An exception is made for a wine drinker: his su'r immediately after drinking is impure due to the impurity in his mouth, but after swallowing saliva three times, it becomes pure. The passage then discusses the purification of impure parts by licking or sucking, contrasting the views of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (who permit removal of impurity by means other than water) with Muhammad (who requires water). The author provides hadith proofs for the purity of human su'r: the hadith of the Prophet drinking from the same cup as a Bedouin, and the hadith of 'A'isha drinking while menstruating, then passing the cup to the Prophet. The su'r of the horse is pure and purifying according to the apparent narration, though there is a second narration. The su'r of halal animals is similarly pure. The passage ends with a discussion on the su'r of a wine drinker and a note on the difference in the cause of dislike for horse meat.

Key Terms
سؤر (su'r): The remnant of liquid left after a person or animal drinks, or any moisture remaining from their mouth.
طهور (tahūr): Pure in itself and purifying for others; water or another liquid that can be used for ritual purification.
نجاسة (najāsah): Impurity; the legal impurity that prevents valid prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the su'r (remnant drink) of a woman who is menstruating?
A) It is impure.
B) It is pure but disliked for purification.
C) It is pure and purifying; dislike only for drinking.
D) It is pure only after she performs ghusl.

Key Takeaway
Human su'r is pure and purifying regardless of the person's state, with the exception of the immediate remnant of a wine drinker.

Taharah — Purification
advanced
10 min
The Remnant of Horses and Edible Animals; Purification by Licking
Rulings on the su'r of horses and halal animals, and the intra-Hanafi dispute on purification by licking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the su'r of horses and halal animals, and compare the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on removing impurity by licking.

Core Idea
Horse su'r is pure and purifying despite the horse being disliked for consumption; halal animal su'r is pure.

This lesson examines the su'r of horses and animals whose meat is eaten. While the horse is disliked for consumption in the Hanafi school, its su'r remains pure due to the reason for dislike being honor, not impurity. The lesson also covers a practical intra-Hanafi dispute: can impurities be removed by licking (saliva) when water is scarce?

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on horse su'r
The matn includes the horse in the general ruling that its su'r is pure. The commentator mentions two narrations from Abu Hanifa; the apparent narration (and the position of the two companions) is that it is pure and purifying without dislike.
2
Understand the reason for the horse's inclusion
The horse is mentioned separately because the cause of dislike of its meat is different—honor for its role in jihad, not impurity. This clarifies that its su'r remains pure.
This is a qiyas: since the flesh is not impure, the su'r is not impure.
3
Note the ruling on halal animal su'r
Animals whose meat is eaten have pure su'r by consensus in the Hanafi school.
4
Analyze the ikhtilaf on purification by licking
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf permit removing impurity from a limb or mouth by licking until the trace is gone, based on istihsan. Muhammad requires water. The analogy is made to a wine drinker whose mouth becomes pure by saliva.
This is a branching from the general permission to remove impurity with non-water substances.

قوله (وسؤر الآدمي والفرس وما يؤكل لحمه طاهر) أما الآدمي فلأن لعابه متولد من لحم طاهر وإنما لا يؤكل لكرامته ولا فرق بين الجنب والطاهر والحائض والنفساء والصغير والكبير والمسلم والكافر والذكر والأنثى. كذا ذكر الزيلعي رحمه الله. يعني أن الكل طاهر طهور من غير كراهة وفيه نظر، فقد صرح في المجتبى من باب الحظر والإباحة أنه يكره سؤر المرأة للرجل وسؤره لها ولهذا لم يذكر الذكر والأنثى في كثير من الكتب لكن قد يقال : الكراهة المذكورة إنما هو في الشرب لا في الطهارة واستثنوا من هذا العموم سؤر شارب الخمر إذا شرب من ساعته فإن سؤره نجس لا لنجاسة لحمه بل لنجاسة فمه كما لو أدمى فوه، أما لو مكث قدر ما يغسل فمه بلعابه ثم شرب لا ينجس . كذا في كثير من الكتب . يقال : إن المعنى أنه ركب حال كونه معرورياً الحمار فهو اسم فاعل من اعروري المتعدي حذف مفعوله للعلم به قوله : ( ولهذا قال في المستصفى الخ ظاهره أن الشك في العرق واللعاب نفسهما فيكون الشك في طهارتهما إذ لا طهورية فيهما إلا أن يحمل على أن المراد الماء الذي أصابه العرق، واللعاب مشكوك فيه أي في طهوريته تأمل. قوله : أنه يكره سؤر المرأة للرجل وسؤره لها قال الرملي أقول: يجب تقييده بغير الزوجة والمحارم وسيأتي حديث عائشة رضي الله تعالى عنها مصرحاً بالأولى. قوله : (إنما هو في الشرب لا في الطهارة أي ليس لعدم طهارته بل للاستلذاذ الحاصل للشارب أثر صاحبه قوله : (أما لو مكث قدر ما يغسل فمه بلعابه الخ) قال في النهر : حتى لو وفي الخلاصة والتنجيس : رجل شرب الخمر إن تردد في فيه من البزاق بحيث لو كان ذلك الخمر على ثوب طهرها البزاق طهر فمه أهـ. وهذا هو الصحيح من مذهب أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ويسقط اعتبار الصب عند أبي يوسف للضرورة ونظيره لو أصاب عضوه نجاسة فلحسها حتى لم يبق أثرها، أو قاء الصغير على ثدي أمه ثم مصه حتى زال الأثر ظهر خلافاً لمحمد في جميعها بناء على عدم جواز إزالة النجاسة بغير الماء المطلق كما سيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى. وفي بعض شروح القدوري : فإن كان شارب الشارب طويلاً ينجس الماء وإن شرب بعد ساعات لأن الشعر الطويل لما تنجس لا يطهر باللسان اهـ. وكأنه لأنه لا يتمكن اللسان من استيعابه بإصابة بله إياه بريقه ثم أخذ ما عليه من البلة النجسة مرة بعد أخرى وإلا فهو ليس دون الشفتين والفم في تطهيره بالريق تفريعاً على قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف في جواز التطهير من النجاسة بغير الماء. كذا في شرح منية المصلي. فإن قيل: أن ينبغي يتنجس سؤر الجنب على القول بنجاسة المستعمل لسقوط الفرض به قلنا : ما يلاقي الماء من فمه مشروب سلمنا أنه ليس بمشروب لكن لحاجة فلا يستعمل به كإدخال يده في الحب إخراج كوزه على ما قدمناه في المياه وقد نقلوا روايتين في رفع الحدث بهذا الشرب، وظاهر كلامهم ترجيح أنه رافع فلا يصير الماء مستعملاً للحرج لكن صرح يعقوب باشا بأن الصحيح أن الفرض لا يسقط به ويدل على طهارة سؤر الآدمي مطلقاً ما رواه مالك من طريق الزهري عن أنس مالك أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أتي بلبن قد شيب بماء بن وعن يمينه أعرابي وعن يساره أبو بكر فشرب ثم أعطى الإعرابي وقال: الأيمن فالأيمن. وروى مسلم وغيره عن عائشة رضي الله عنها قالت كنت أشرب وأنا حائض فأناوله النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فيضع فاه على موضع في. ولما أنزل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بعض المشركين في المسجد ومكنه من المبيت فيه على ما في الصحيحين علم أن المراد بقوله تعالى إنما المشركون نجس [التوبة : ٢٨] النجاسة في اعتقادهم وقد روي أن النبي ل لقي حذيفة فمد يده ليصافحه فقبض يده وقال : إني جنب فقال عليه السلام المؤمن ليس بنجس. ذكره البغوي في المصابيح. وأما سؤر الفرس ففيه روايتان عن أبي حنيفة ؛ فظاهر الرواية عنه طهوريته من غير كراهة وهو قولهما لأن كراهة لحمه عنده لاحترامه لأنه آلة الجهاد لا لنجاسته فلا يؤثر في كراهة سؤره وهو الصحيح. كذا في البدائع وغيره. وأما سؤر ما يؤكل لحمه فلأنه شرب بعد شربه الخمر فوراً كان سؤره نجساً إلا أن يبلع ريقه ثلاثاً عند الإمام. قيل : والثاني ويسقط اشتراط الصب في هذه الحالة والتقييد بالثلاث جرى عليه كثير . قوله : (لكن صرح يعقوب باشا بأن الصحيح أن الفرض لا يسقط به قال في النهر والأوّل أولى قوله : (وأما سؤر الفرس) قال في النهر : وخصها بالذكر وإن دخلت فيما يؤكل لحمه للاختلاف في علة الكراهة وإن كانت على الظاهر
His statement: (And the remnant of the human, the horse, and what is eaten for its meat is pure). As for the human, because his saliva is generated from pure flesh and it is only uneaten due to his honor, and there is no difference between the junub and the pure, the menstruating woman, the postpartum woman, the child and the adult, the Muslim and the disbeliever, the male and the female. Thus mentioned al-Zayla'i (may Allah have mercy on him). Meaning that all are pure and purifying without dislike, but there is consideration. For it is stated in al-Mujtaba, in the chapter on prohibition and permission, that the remnant of a woman is disliked for a man and his remnant for her; for this reason male and female were not mentioned in many books. But it may be said: The mentioned dislike is only in drinking, not in purification. And they excepted from this generality the remnant of a wine drinker if he drinks immediately; his remnant is impure not because of his flesh but because of the impurity of his mouth, as if he bled from his mouth. But if he waits enough time to wash his mouth with his saliva then drinks, it does not become impure. Thus in many books. It is said: The meaning is that he mounted while being bareback on the donkey; it is a participle from 'i'rawara' transitive, its object omitted due to being known. His statement: (And for this reason it is said in al-Mustasfa...) its apparent is that the doubt is in the sweat and saliva themselves, so the doubt is in their purity, as there is no purifying quality except if it is taken to mean the water that the sweat or saliva touched, i.e., its purifying quality is doubted. Ponder. His statement: (That the remnant of the woman is disliked for the man and his remnant for her) al-Ramli said: I say it must be restricted to other than the wife and mahrams, and the hadith of 'A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her) will come explicitly regarding the first. His statement: (It is only in drinking, not in purification) meaning not due to lack of purity but due to the pleasure the drinker obtains from the trace of the other. His statement: (But if he waits enough to wash his mouth with his saliva etc.) al-Nahr said: ...even if... And in al-Khulasa and al-Tanjis: A man drank wine; if there remains in his mouth spittle such that if that wine were on a garment the spittle would purify it, then his mouth becomes purified. This is the correct according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and the requirement of pouring is waived according to Abu Yusuf due to necessity. Its analogy: If an impurity touches his limb and he licks it until no trace remains, or a child vomits on his mother's breast and she sucks it until the trace disappears, it becomes pure, contrary to Muhammad in all these based on the impermissibility of removing impurity with other than absolute water, as will come. In some commentaries of al-Quduri: If the beard of the drinker is long, it makes the water impure even if he drinks after hours because the long hair, having become impure, is not purified by the tongue. It seems because the tongue cannot encompass all of it by moistening it with his saliva and then taking the wetness of the impurity repeatedly, otherwise it is not less than the lips and mouth in purification by saliva, as a branching from the statement of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the permissibility of removing impurity with other than water. Thus in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli. If it is said: The remnant of a junub should become impure according to the view that used water is impure because the duty is dropped by it, we say: What contacts the water from his mouth is drunk; we concede it is not drunk, but it is for need so it is not used by him, like inserting his hand into the jar to take out his cup, as we have mentioned in the chapter on waters. They have transmitted two narrations regarding the removal of hadath by this drinking; the apparent of their speech is a preference that it is removive, so the water does not become used due to hardship, but Ya'qub Pasha stated clearly that the correct is that the duty does not fall by it. And evidence for the purity of the human's remnant absolutely is what Malik narrated through al-Zuhri from Anas that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was brought milk mixed with water, and on his right was a Bedouin and on his left was Abu Bakr; he drank then gave it to the Bedouin and said: 'The rightmost, then the rightmost.' And Muslim and others narrated from 'A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her) she said: 'I used to drink while I was menstruating, and I would hand it to the Prophet (peace be upon him) and he would place his mouth on the place of mine.' And when the Prophet (peace be upon him) lodged some polytheists in the mosque and allowed them to stay overnight, as in the Sahihs, it is known that the intention of His statement 'Indeed the polytheists are impure' (9:28) is impurity in their beliefs. And it is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) met Hudhayfah and extended his hand to shake hands; Hudhayfah withdrew his hand and said: 'I am junub,' so he said: 'The believer is not impure.' Mentioned by al-Baghawi in al-Masabih. As for the remnant of the horse, there are two narrations from Abu Hanifa: the apparent narration is that it is purifying without dislike, and this is the statement of the two companions because the dislike of its meat according to him is due to respect because it is an instrument of jihad, not due to its impurity, so it does not affect the dislike of its remnant, and this is the correct. Thus in al-Bada'i' and others. As for the remnant of what is eaten for its meat, because he drank after drinking wine immediately, his remnant is impure unless he swallows his saliva three times according to the Imam. It is said: The second (narrator?) and the condition of pouring is waived in this case, and the restriction to three is applied by many. His statement: (But Ya'qub Pasha stated clearly that the correct is that the duty does not fall by it) al-Nahr said and the first is more appropriate. His statement: (As for the remnant of the horse) al-Nahr said: He specified it although it is included in what is eaten for its meat due to the difference in the cause of dislike, even if it is apparently...
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement about the su'r (remnant) of humans, horses, and halal animals being pure. The author first explains that human su'r is pure because it originates from pure flesh; no distinction is made between various states of the person (junub, menstruating, etc.) or between men and women. However, the author notes a disagreement: some sources dislike the su'r of a woman for a man (and vice versa) but this is restricted to drinking, not purification. An exception is made for a wine drinker: his su'r immediately after drinking is impure due to the impurity in his mouth, but after swallowing saliva three times, it becomes pure. The passage then discusses the purification of impure parts by licking or sucking, contrasting the views of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (who permit removal of impurity by means other than water) with Muhammad (who requires water). The author provides hadith proofs for the purity of human su'r: the hadith of the Prophet drinking from the same cup as a Bedouin, and the hadith of 'A'isha drinking while menstruating, then passing the cup to the Prophet. The su'r of the horse is pure and purifying according to the apparent narration, though there is a second narration. The su'r of halal animals is similarly pure. The passage ends with a discussion on the su'r of a wine drinker and a note on the difference in the cause of dislike for horse meat.

Key Terms
سؤر (su'r): The remnant of liquid left after a person or animal drinks.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a legal method that allows departing from strict qiyas in favor of a more equitable ruling.
نجاسة (najāsah): Impurity; the legal impurity that prevents valid prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the su'r of a horse?
A) It is impure.
B) It is pure but disliked for use in purification.
C) It is pure and purifying without dislike.
D) It depends on whether the horse was used in jihad.

Key Takeaway
Horse su'r is pure; purification by licking is permitted by Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, providing flexibility in removing impurities without water.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
10 min
Ruling on Su'ur of Dogs, Pigs, and Beasts of Prey: Hanafi Position and Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute with hadith analysis and abrogation argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the su'ur of a dog and explain the three-wash position, citing the hadith evidence and the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i seven-wash position.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school rules that a dog's leftover water is impure and the vessel must be washed three times, abrogating the earlier seven-wash command.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author expands on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the su'ur of dogs, pigs, and beasts of prey. After clarifying the grammatical reading of the matn, he enters into the well-known inter-madhhab dispute on the number of washes after a dog drinks from a vessel. This lesson focuses on the Hanafi argument for three washes, the counterargument from the Shafi'i school, and the Hanafi response based on abrogation and the practice of Abu Hurayra.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What does the Hanafi school rule regarding the su'ur of a dog? Is it pure or impure? How many times must the vessel be washed?
Hanafi: su'ur is impure, vessel washed 3 times.
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
Which other school(s) differ on this ruling? What is their position on the impurity and the number of washes?
Shafi'i: impure, 7 washes with earth. Maliki: pure, 7 washes devotional.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What evidence does the Shafi'i school cite for seven washes? Is it direct from the Prophet?
Hadith of Abu Hurayra commanding seven washes, one with earth, narrated by the six imams.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut the seven-wash hadith? What evidence do they present for three washes?
They cite a hadith of three washes from Abu Hurayra (mawquf and marfu') and argue abrogation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in rulings between the schools in terms of purification requirements?
Hanafi: 3 washes, no earth. Shafi'i: 7 washes, one with earth. Maliki: 7 washes devotional.
