Using Limited Water: Hadath or Filth Priority
Ruling on using scarce water for hadath vs filth removal, with intra-Hanafi nuances

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when water is sufficient for only hadath or filth removal, and identify the legal reasoning (qiyas) used to prioritize filth removal over hadath.

Core Idea
Water owed to filth is legally non-existent for hadath — tayammum substitutes for hadath, not filth.

Imagine you have only enough water to either make wudu or wash a spot of filth off your garment. Which one takes priority? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the Hanafi ruling: the water must be used to remove the filth, and you perform tayammum for hadath. We'll unpack the reasoning and the intra-Hanafi disagreement about the order of actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that the person has water sufficient for only one of two obligations: removing hadath (ritual impurity) or removing najasa (filth).
The water is scarce, not sufficient for both.
2
State the primary ruling
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars (and others), one should use the water to wash the filth and then perform tayammum for the hadath.
If he does the opposite (wudu first), his prayer is valid but sinful.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
Examine the disagreement between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding the order: if he does tayammum first, does it need to be repeated after washing filth?
Muhammad says repeat; Abu Yusuf says may choose. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq prefers permissibility.
4
Analyze the qiyas
Understand the analogy to the case of a junub with a dry spot (lum'a) after using all water: the water is legally considered non-existent for the purpose of tayammum because it is owed (mustahaqq) to the filth.
This qiyas justifies the permissibility of tayammum even though water exists physically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وجد ماء يكفي للحدث أو إزالة النجاسة المانعة غسل به الثوب منها وتيمم للحدث عند عامة العلماء، وإن توضأ به وصلى في النجس أجزأه وكان مسيئاً. كذا في الخانية. وفي المحيط : ولو تيمم أولاً ثم غسل النجاسة يعيد التيمم لأنه تيمم وهو قادر على ما يتوضأ به ا هـ. وفيه نظر بل الظاهر الحكم بجواز التيمم تقدم على غسل الثوب أو تأخر لأنه مستحق الصرف إلى الثوب على ما قالوا والمستحق الصرف إلى جهة معدوم حكماً بالنسبة إلى غيرها كما في مسألة اللمعة مع بطاهر من جنس الأرض. قوله : (كما في مسألة اللمعة أي لو اغتسل الجنب وفرغ ماؤه ثم علم أنه بقيت منه لمعة من جسده لم يصبها الماء فإنه يتيمم لها لأنه لم يخرج عن الجنابة، ولو أحدث قبل أن يتيمم لها فإنه يتيمم تيمماً واحداً لها وللحدث، وإذا أحدث بعد التيمم ثم وجد ماء يكفي لكل واحد منهما على الانفراد غسل به اللمعة لأن الجنابة أغلظ ثم يتيمم للحدث، ولو بدأ بالتيمم ثم غسلها في رواية لا يجوز ويعيد التيمم وفي رواية له أن يبدأ بأيهما شاء؛ قيل الأولى قول محمد، والثانية قول أبي يوسف وفي المسألة تفاصيل بينها في السراج . وقد ذكر في السراج مسألة النجاسة بعد هذه وقال : لو بدأ بالتيمم أولاً ثم غسل النجاسة أعاد التيمم إجماعاً بخلاف مسألة الأولى أي مسألة اللمعة على قول أبي يوسف لأنه تيمم هنا وهو قادر على ماء لو توضأ به جاز، وهناك أي في مسألة اللمعة لو توضأ بذلك الماء لم يجز لأنه عاد جنباً برؤية الماء ا هـ. وبه يندفع النظر فتدبر قوله : (والفرسخ اثنا عشر ألف خطوة الخ) قال الرملي : هذا مخالف لما في الزيلعي والجوهرة أن قدر الميل أربعة آلاف ذراع، والذي هنا ستة آلاف ذراع ورأيت في القلادة الجوهرية ما صورته قال صاحبنا أبو العباس أحمد شهاب الدين بن الهائم رحمه الله : وإليه يرجع في هذا الباب البريد أربعة فراسخ، والفراسخ ثلاثة أميال والميل ألف باع والباع أربعة أذرع، والذراع أربعة وعشرون أصبعاً، والأصبع ست
And if he finds water sufficient either for the hadath or for removing preventing filth, he washes the garment from it and performs tayammum for the hadath according to the majority of scholars. If he makes wudu with it and prays in the impure garment, it is valid but he is sinful. Thus in al-Khaniyya. In al-Muhit: If he performs tayammum first and then washes the filth, he must repeat the tayammum because he performed tayammum while being capable of water for wudu. There is a consideration in this; rather the apparent ruling is the permissibility of tayammum whether it precedes or follows washing the garment, because the water is due to be spent on the garment as they said, and the due to be spent on a certain direction is nonexistent in relation to other directions, as in the case of the spot (lum'a) with pure earth. His statement: (as in the case of the spot) meaning if a junub bathes and his water runs out, then he knows that a spot of his body remained dry, he performs tayammum for it because he has not left the state of janaba. If he had hadath before performing tayammum for it, he performs one tayammum for both. If he had hadath after tayammum and then finds water sufficient for each separately, he washes the spot because janaba is more severe, then performs tayammum for the hadath. If he begins with tayammum then washes it, in one narration it is not valid and he repeats tayammum; in another narration he may begin with whichever. It is said the first is the opinion of Muhammad, the second of Abu Yusuf, and there are details for this case in al-Siraj. In al-Siraj, the case of filth is mentioned after this and he said: If he begins with tayammum first then washes the filth, he repeats tayammum by consensus, unlike the first case (lum'a) according to Abu Yusuf because he performed tayammum here while capable of water with which he could have made wudu validly, while there in the lum'a case if he made wudu with that water it would be invalid because he becomes junub upon seeing water. With this the consideration is dispelled, so ponder. His statement: (and a parasang is twelve thousand steps). Al-Ramli said: This contradicts what is in al-Zaylai and al-Jawhara that the mile is four thousand cubits, while here it is six thousand cubits. I saw in al-Qalada al-Jawhariyya: Our companion Abu al-Abbas Ahmad Shihab al-Din ibn al-Ha'im said: In this chapter, a post is four parasangs, a parasang is three miles, a mile is a thousand fathoms, a fathom is four cubits, a cubit is twenty-four fingers, a finger is six [barleycorns].
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage deals with the scenario where a person has water sufficient only for purification from hadath (wudu/ghusl) or from filth (najasa). The Hanafi ruling is that one should use the water to remove the filth and perform tayammum for the hadath. This is because the removal of filth requires water in absolute terms, while for hadath, tayammum can substitute. The majority of scholars agree. If he does the opposite (makes wudu with the water and then prays with the filth on his clothes), the prayer is valid but he is sinful. There is a discussion about the order: if he performs tayammum first, then washes the filth, some say he must repeat the tayammum, but the author argues that the apparent view is that it is permissible because the water was destined for the filth, and for the purpose of tayammum, that water is considered non-existent. This is similar to the case of a junub who has a dry spot (lum'a) on his body: he must perform tayammum for that spot. If he then needs to also remove hadath, he combines them. The passage also includes an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether to begin with tayammum or washing the filth: according to Muhammad, one must repeat tayammum if he starts with tayammum; according to Abu Yusuf, he may choose. The author then quotes al-Siraj clarifying that in the case of filth, there is consensus that tayammum must be repeated if done first, unlike the lum'a case on Abu Yusuf's opinion. Finally, the text addresses the definition of a mile, with a quote from al-Ramli noting a discrepancy: some say 4,000 cubits, others 6,000. A detailed chain from Ibn al-Ha'im gives a different measure. This is important for determining the threshold distance for tayammum.

Key Terms
الحدث (al-hadath): State of ritual impurity (minor or major) requiring wudu or ghusl.
النجاسة (al-najasa): Filth on the body, clothing, or place of prayer that prevents validity.
اللمعة (al-lum'a): A dry spot left on the body after incomplete ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person has water sufficient only for either making wudu or washing a spot of filth from his garment, what should he do?
A) He should use the water for wudu and pray with the filth, as wudu is more important.
B) He should wash the filth with the water and perform tayammum for wudu.
C) He should split the water and do both partially.
D) He should perform tayammum for both.

Key Takeaway
When water is insufficient for both hadath and filth removal, Hanafi fiqh prioritizes removing filth with water and uses tayammum for hadath, based on the principle that water owed to filth is legally non-existent for hadath.

Order of Tayammum and Washing the Lum'a: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on order of tayammum and washing lum'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two intra-Hanafi narrations regarding the order of tayammum and washing the lum'a, and explain the practical consequence of each.

Core Idea
When water is scarce, the order of tayammum and washing the lum'a differs between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses a scenario where a person has both a remaining unpurified spot (lum'a) from janabah and minor impurity (hadath), but only enough water for one purification. The Hanafi school has two narrations on the correct order, attributed to Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf. Understanding this dispute reveals how the school balances the severity of janabah with procedural validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize the case: a person has a lum'a (unwashed spot from janabah) and also hadath (minor impurity), with water sufficient for only one of the two purifications.
The water is not enough for both ghusl and wudu; it covers only one act.
2
Default principle
Know that the default ruling allows performing tayammum for hadath before washing the lum'a, analogous to using water for thirst when purification is not possible.
This is based on the principle of necessity.
3
Analyze the two narrations
Understand the dispute: one narration (Imam Muhammad) holds that if tayammum for hadath is done before washing the lum'a, the tayammum is invalid and must be repeated. The other narration (Imam Abu Yusuf) holds that the order is flexible and valid.
The difference stems from whether the lum'a impedes purification from hadath.
4
Understand the resolution from al-Siraj
Note the distinction: in the case of najasah (physical impurity), starting with tayammum then washing najasah invalidates tayammum by consensus because water was usable for wudu. In the lum'a case, Abu Yusuf's view holds because the water would not have been valid for wudu (seeing water after tayammum reinstates janabah).
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمسح هـ. رواية الحدث قبل التيمم له إذا كان الماء كافياً لأحدهما فبدأ بالتيمم للحدث قبل غسلها كما هو الأصل، وكالماء المستحق للعطش ونحوه . نعم يتمشى ذلك على رواية الزيادات القائلة بأنه لو تيمم قبل غسل اللمعة لا يصح والله سبحانه أعلم. بقيت منه لمع من جسده لم يصبها الماء فإنه يتيمم لها لأنه لم يخرج عن الجنابة، ولو أحدث قبل أن يتيمم لها فإنه يتيمم تيمماً واحداً لها وللحدث، وإذا أحدث بعد التيمم ثم وجد ماء يكفي لكل واحد منهما على الانفراد غسل به اللمعة لأن الجنابة أغلظ ثم يتيمم للحدث، ولو بدأ بالتيمم ثم غسلها في رواية لا يجوز ويعيد التيمم وفي رواية له أن يبدأ بأيهما شاء؛ قيل الأولى قول محمد، والثانية قول أبي يوسف وفي المسألة تفاصيل بينها في السراج . وقد ذكر في السراج مسألة النجاسة بعد هذه وقال : لو بدأ بالتيمم أولاً : غسل النجاسة أعاد التيمم إجماعاً بخلاف مسألة الأولى أي مسألة اللمعة على قول أبي يوسف لأنه تيمم هنا وهو قادر على ماء لو توضأ به جاز، وهناك أي في مسألة اللمعة لو توضأ بذلك الماء لم يجز لأنه عاد جنباً برؤية الماء ا هـ. وبه يندفع النظر فتدبر
He wipes... narration: he performs tayammum for hadath before washing it (the lum'a) as is the principle, and like water needed for thirst etc. Yes, this is consistent with the narration of al-Ziyadat that if he performed tayammum before washing the lum'a, it is not valid, and Allah knows best. If a piece (of his body) remains from his body that water did not reach, he performs tayammum for it because he has not left janabah. If he has hadath before performing tayammum for it, he performs one tayammum for it and for the hadath. If he has hadath after tayammum and then finds water sufficient for each separately, he washes the lum'a with it because janabah is more severe, then he performs tayammum for hadath. If he began with tayammum and then washed it, in one narration it is not valid and he repeats tayammum; in another narration he may begin with whichever he wishes. It is said the first is the view of Muhammad, the second of Abu Yusuf, and there are details of this ruling in al-Siraj. Al-Siraj mentioned the case of najasah after this and said: If he began first with tayammum and then washed the najasah, he repeats tayammum by consensus, unlike the first case (the case of the lum'a) according to Abu Yusuf, because here he performed tayammum while being able to use water for wudu, whereas there (in the lum'a case) if he performed wudu with that water it would not be valid because he becomes junub upon seeing the water. This refutes the objection, so reflect.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the order of purification when a person has both janabah (a remaining unpurified spot called lum'a) and hadath (minor impurity), and water is sufficient for only one of the two. The base principle is that if water is enough for one purpose, one may perform tayammum for hadath before washing the lum'a, akin to using water for thirst. However, a conflicting narration from al-Ziyadat states that if one performs tayammum before washing the lum'a, it is invalid. The text then explains the scenario: if a person has a lum'a from janabah, he must perform tayammum for it because he is still in a state of janabah. If he has hadath before tayammum, he performs one tayammum for both. After tayammum, if he finds water sufficient for only one, he washes the lum'a first because janabah is graver, then performs tayammum for hadath. The dispute arises when he starts with tayammum then washes the lum'a: one narration (attributed to Muhammad) says he must repeat tayammum; another (attributed to Abu Yusuf) says he may start with whichever he wishes. The author clarifies that according to al-Siraj, the case of najasah (ritual impurity on the body) is different: if one starts with tayammum then washes the najasah, he repeats tayammum by consensus, because at the time of tayammum he had access to water that would have sufficed for wudu. In the lum'a case, however, Abu Yusuf argues that the water would not have been valid for wudu because seeing water after tayammum renders one junub again (a separate ruling). This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Key Terms
اللمعة (al-lum'a): A spot or area of the body that remains unpurified after a partial washing from janabah.
الحدث (al-hadath): Minor ritual impurity requiring wudu or tayammum, e.g., breaking wind, sleep.
الجنابة (al-janabah): Major ritual impurity requiring ghusl or tayammum, e.g., after sexual intercourse or emission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is always invalid and must be repeated
B) It is valid according to both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
C) There are two narrations: one says invalid (Muhammad), another says permissible (Abu Yusuf)
D) It is only valid if the water is for thirst, not for purification

Key Takeaway
In scarcity of water, the order of tayammum and washing the lum'a is disputed; Muhammad requires lum'a first, Abu Yusuf allows either.

Two Types of Water Absence and the Mile Criterion
Defines two types of absence and the mile criterion for tayammum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two types of 'adam (absence) for tayammum and identify the relied-upon distance measure for actual absence in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The absence of water for tayammum is either physical distance or legal incapacity; the chosen measure for distance is one mile (one-third farsakh).

The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra-iq explains that tayammum is permitted only when water is genuinely absent. This absence can be actual (water is far away) or virtual (water is present but cannot be used). The Hanafi school had several opinions on the exact distance that constitutes actual absence, but the relied-upon view is one mile, as it avoids unnecessary hardship. This lesson breaks down these definitions and the preferred ruling.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of 'adam
Identify that absence of water is either physical (al-'adam al-suri) or legal/virtual (al-'adam al-ma'nawi). Physical absence means water is not within reach due to distance; legal absence means water is present but using it is impossible or harmful (e.g., fear of thirst, missing the caravan).
The text explicitly states: 'والعدم على نوعان: عدم من حيث الصورة والمعنى' — absence in form and meaning.
2
Identify the distance criteria for actual absence
Three criteria are mentioned: (1) Imam Muhammad: one mile; (2) Al-Karkhi: the range of hearing voices of water-owners; (3) Abu Yusuf: whether going to fetch water would cause the caravan to depart. Note the textual sources for each.
The preferred measure is the mile as per al-Hidayah: 'والميل هو المختار في المقدار'.
3
Understand the definition of a mile in Hanafi jurisprudence
A mile is defined as one-third of a farsakh. A farsakh equals 12,000 steps, each step being 1.5 cubits (24 fingers). Thus a mile is 4,000 cubits, approximately 2 kilometers.
The text references al-Yanabi' for the conversion.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
If a person is certain that water is a mile or more away, tayammum is permissible. If it is less, or if he merely thinks it is a mile or less, tayammum is not permissible. For virtual absence, the same permissibility applies when legal impediments exist.
Certainty (tahaqquq) is required for the mile; mere assumption (zann) is insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعدم على نوعان: عدم من حيث الصورة والمعنى، وعدم من حيث المعنى لا من حيث الصورة، فالأول أن يكون بعيداً عنه قال في البدائع : ولم يذكر حد البعد في ظاهر الروايات ؛ فعن محمد التقدير بالميل فإن تحقق كونه ميلاً جاز له التيمم، وإن تحقق كونه أقل أو ظن أنه ميل أو أقل لا يجوز قال في الهداية : والميل هو المختار في المقدار لأن يلحقه الحرج بدخول المصر والماء معدوم حقيقة. والميل في كلام العرب منتهى مد البصر، وقيل للأعلام المبنية في طريق مكة أميال لأنها بنيت على مقادير منتهى البصر . كذا في الصحاح والمغرب. والمراد هنا ثلث الفرسخ والفرسخ أثنا عشر ألف خطوة، كل خطوة ذراع ونصف بذراع العامة، وهو أربع وعشرون أصبعاً. كذا في الينابيع. وعن الكرخي رحمه الله أنه إن كان في موضع يسمع صوت أهل الماء فهو قريب، وإن كان لا فهو بعيد وبه أخذ أكثر مشايخنا. كذا في الخانية. وعن أبي يوسف : إذا كان يسمع. بحيث لو ذهب إليه وتوضأ تذهب القافلة وتغيب عن بصره فهو بعيد ويجوز له التيمم، واستحسن المشايخ هذه الرواية كذا في التنجيس وغيره إلا أن ظاهره أنه في حق المسافر لا المقيم وهو جائز لهما لو في المصر لأن الشرط هو العدم فأينما تحقق جاز التيمم.
And absence is of two types: absence in form and meaning, and absence in meaning not in form. The first is that he is far from it (water). Al-Bada'i said: The limit of distance is not mentioned in the apparent narrations; from Muhammad it is estimated by a mile. If it is certain it is a mile, tayammum is permissible; if it is certain it is less, or he thinks it is a mile or less, it is not permissible. Al-Hidayah said: The mile is chosen as the measure because otherwise he would suffer hardship entering the city while water is essentially absent. The mile in Arab speech is the limit of sight, and the landmarks built on the road to Mecca are called amyal because they were built at distances of the limit of sight. This is in al-Sihah and al-Mughrib. What is meant here is one-third of a farsakh, and a farsakh is twelve thousand steps, each step is a cubit and a half of the general cubit, which is twenty-four fingers. This is in al-Yanabi'. From al-Karkhi: If he is in a place where he can hear the voices of the people of water, it is near; if not, it is far, and this is adopted by most of our masters. This is in al-Khaniyyah. From Abu Yusuf: If he hears such that if he goes to it and performs wudu, the caravan would leave and disappear from his sight, it is far, and tayammum is permissible, and the masters deemed this narration good, as in al-Tanjees and others, except that its apparent meaning is concerning the traveler, not the resident; but it is permissible for both in the city because the condition is absence, so wherever it is realized, tayammum is permissible.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage defines the condition of 'absence of water' for tayammum. The author explains that absence is of two kinds: actual (water is far away) and virtual (water is present but cannot be used). For actual absence, the key is distance. The text notes that the earlier Hanafi texts (Zahir al-Riwayah) did not specify a precise distance. Imam Muhammad estimated it as one mile. Al-Hidayah confirms that one mile is the chosen measure because it avoids hardship. Philologically, a mile is defined as the limit of eyesight or markers on the Mecca road. In Hanafi jurisprudence, a mile is one-third of a farsakh, where a farsakh equals 12,000 steps, each step being 1.5 cubits (24 fingers). There is also a criterion from al-Karkhi based on hearing: if one can hear the voices of the water-owners, it is near; otherwise far. Most Hanafi scholars adopt this. Additionally, a narration from Abu Yusuf considers whether going to fetch water would cause one to miss the caravan; if so, it is far. This narration is praised by later masters, but it may apply primarily to travelers. The overarching principle is that permissibility depends on the reality of absence, not on the type of person (traveler or resident).

Key Terms
العدم (al-'adam): The absence of water that permits tayammum; can be actual (physical distance) or virtual (inability to use).
الميل (al-mil): A unit of distance defined as the limit of eyesight or one-third of a farsakh; chosen as the standard for distance absence of water.
الفرسخ (al-farsakh): A distance unit of three miles, or twelve thousand steps.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relied-upon measure for distance when determining if water is absent for tayammum?
A) The distance from which the caravan departs
B) One farsakh (twelve thousand steps)
C) One mile (one-third farsakh)
D) The range of hearing the voices of water-owners

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the absence of water for tayammum is established by a distance of at least one mile or by a legal impediment that prevents its use.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
6 min
The Criterion for Tayammum: Distance vs. Fear of Missing Time
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tayammum basis: distance vs. fear of missing time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi criterion for tayammum and explain why the fear of missing prayer time is not a valid basis, distinguishing Zufar's opposing view.

Core Idea
Tayammum is permitted by travel distance, not by fear of missing time — Zufar's contrary view is a rare transmission.

This passage clarifies a key intra-Hanafi dispute between the majority Hanafi position and Zufar regarding the basis for tayammum when water is present but time is short. The text establishes that the sole criterion is the distance of travel, not the fear of missing the prayer time. We will explore the evidence, practical implications, and the status of the opposing narration.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school (Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) holds that tayammum is only valid when water is physically absent or the person is a traveler beyond a certain distance. Fear of missing the prayer time alone does not justify tayammum.
The text explicitly says 'المعتبر المسافة دون خوف فوت الوقت' (the consideration is distance, not fear of missing time).
2
Identify Zufar's opposing view
Zufar considers 'khawf fawt al-waqt' (fear of missing the prayer time) as a valid reason for tayammum, even if water is available. This is supported by a narration in al-Mubtagha.
Al-Mubtagha mentions tayammum for fear of fleas, rain, or missing time, which aligns with Zufar.
3
Analyze the author's tarjih (preference)
The author (Ibn Nujaym) sides with the majority. He notes that the opposing narration is rare (nawadir) and not the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). The examples of the crowd at the well and the naked men sharing a garment demonstrate that waiting is preferred over tayammum when fear of missing time is the only issue.
The author states: 'وهذا مناسب لقول زفر لا لقول أئمتنا' (this suits Zufar's view, not our imams).
4
Apply to practical scenarios
Work through the examples: (1) Crowd at a well: if turn comes after time, Hanafi says do wudu after time; Zufar says tayammum. (2) Naked men sharing a garment: wait rather than pray naked. (3) Patient unable to stand: wait and pray standing after time.
These examples reinforce that the school prioritizes proper purification over performing the prayer within the time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حر شديد إن الأسرار لكن قال في شرح الطحاوي: لا يجوز التيمم في المصر إلا لخوف فوت جنازة أو صلاة عيد أو للجنب الخائف من البرد، وكذا ذكر التمرتاشي بناء على كونه نادراً والحق الأول لما ذكرنا والمنع بناء على عادة الأمصار فليس خلافاً حقيقياً، وتصحيح الزيلعي لا يفيده. وفي الخانية؛ قليل السفر وكثير سواء في التيمم والصلاة على الدابة خارج المصر إنما الفرق بين القليل والكثير في ثلاثة : في قصر الصلاة والإفطار والمسح على الخفين ا هـ . وفي المحيط : المسافر يطأ جاريته وإن علم أنه لا يجد الماء لأن التراب شرع طهوراً حالة عدم الماء ولا تكره الجنابة حال وجود الماء فكذا حال عدمه ا هـ. وبما قررناه علم أن المعتبر المسافة دون خوف فوت الوقت خلافاً لزفر . وفي المبتغى بالغين المعجمة : ومن كان في كلة جاز تيممه لخوف البق أو مطر أو خاف فوت الوقت أهـ. ولا يخفى أن هذا مناسب لقول زفر لا لقول أئمتنا فإنهم لا يعتبرون خوف الفوت وإنما العبرة للبعد كما قدمناه كذا في شرح منية المصلي، لكن ظفرت بأن التيمم لخوف فوت الوقت رواية عن مشايخنا ذكرها في القنية في مسائل من ابتلي ببليتين، ويتفرغ على هذا الاختلاف ما لو ازدحم جمع على بئر لا يمكن الاستقاء منها إلا بالمناوبة لضيق الموقف أو لاتحاد الآلة للاستقاء ونحو ذلك، فإن كان يتوقع وصول النوبة إليه قبل خروج الوقت لم يجز له التيمم بالاتفاق، وإن علم أنها لا تصير إليه إلا بعد خروج الوقت عندنا ليتوضأ بعد الوقت، وعند زفر يتيمم ولو كان جمع من العراة وليس معهم إلا ثوب يتناوبونه وعلم أن النوبة لا تصل إليه إلا بعد الوقت فإنه يصبر ولا يصلي عارياً، ولو يصبر اجتمعوا في سفينة أو بيت ضيق وليس هناك موضع يسع أن يصلي قائماً فقط لا يصلي قاعداً بل يصبر ويصلي قائماً بعد الوقت كما لو كان مريضاً عاجزاً عن القيام واستعمال الماء في الوقت ويغلب على ظنه القدرة بعده وكذا لو كان معه ثوب نجس ومعه ماء يغسله ولكن لو غسله خرج الوقت لزم غسله وإن خرج الوقت. كذا في التوشيح. وأما العدم معنى صورة فهو أن : يعجز عن استعمال الماء لمانع مع قرب الماء منه وس وسيأتي بيانه مفصلاً.
Intense heat... but he said in Sharh al-Tahawi: Tayammum is not permissible in the city except for fear of missing a funeral or Eid prayer, or for the junub who fears cold. Similarly al-Tamartashi mentioned it based on it being rare, and the truth is the first because of what we mentioned, and the prohibition is based on the custom of cities, so it is not a real disagreement, and the correction of al-Zayla'i does not benefit it. In al-Khaniyya: Little travel and much travel are equal in tayammum and prayer on an animal outside the city; the difference between little and much is only in three: shortening prayer, breaking fast, and wiping over khuffs. In al-Muhit: The traveler may have intercourse with his slave woman even if he knows he will not find water, because soil is legislated as a purifier in the absence of water, and janaba is not disliked when water exists, so likewise in its absence. By what we have established, it is known that the consideration is distance, not fear of missing time, contrary to Zufar. In al-Mubtagha: Whoever is in a mosquito net, his tayammum is permissible for fear of fleas or rain or fear of missing time. It is not hidden that this is suitable for Zufar's saying, not for the saying of our imams, for they do not consider fear of missing time; rather the criterion is distance, as we have previously mentioned. This is from Sharh Munyat al-Musalli. But I found that tayammum for fear of missing time is a narration from our mashayikh mentioned in al-Qunyah in issues of one afflicted with two calamities. Upon this difference branches what if a crowd gathers at a well and drawing water is only possible by turns due to narrowness of the place or unity of the drawing instrument and the like. If he expects his turn to come before the time ends, tayammum is not permissible by agreement. If he knows it will not come except after the time, according to us he performs wudu after the time, and according to Zufar he performs tayammum. Also if a group of naked men have only one garment which they take turns, and they know his turn will not come except after the time, he should wait and not pray naked. Similarly, if they gather in a ship or narrow house and there is no space to pray standing, he does not pray sitting but waits and prays standing after the time, just as if he were a patient unable to stand or use water in the time and he strongly expects ability afterward. Similarly, if he has a najis garment and water to wash it, but if he washes it the time would exit, he must wash it even if the time exits. Thus in al-Tawshih. As for non-existence in a figurative sense, it means he is unable to use water due to a hindrance despite water being close, and its detailed explanation will come.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute (Hanafi vs Zufar) regarding the basis for permitting tayammum. The author first clarifies that tayammum in the city (misr) is generally not allowed except in specific cases: fear of missing a funeral prayer or Eid prayer, or a junub person fearing extreme cold (which might cause harm). This is based on the custom of cities where water is typically available, so tayammum would be a rare exception. However, the Hanafi position, as explained, is that the essential consideration is the distance of travel (safar), not the fear of missing time. This contrasts with Zufar's view that fear of missing time alone justifies tayammum. The text then provides illustrations: if people crowd around a well and must take turns, and a person knows his turn will come after the prayer time ends, our school (Hanafi) says he must wait and perform wudu after the time, whereas Zufar permits tayammum. Similarly, cases involving a single garment shared among naked men, or a patient unable to stand, reinforce the principle. The passage also notes a narration from some Hanafi mashayikh allowing tayammum for fear of missing time, but this is considered a rare transmission (nawadir), not the relied-upon position. The author emphasizes that the authoritative view is that distance (b'ud) is the criterion, as established in the earlier discussion.

Key Terms
تيمم (tayammum): The act of using clean earth or soil to perform a symbolic purification in place of wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable or harmful.
مصر (misr): A city or town where water is generally available; tayammum is normally not allowed here except for specific exemptions.
سفر قليل وسفر كثير (safar qalil wa safar kathir): Short travel (less than three days) and long travel (three days or more). In the Hanafi school, both permit tayammum and prayer on a mount, but only long travel permits qasr, iftar, and wiping over khuffs.
خوف فوت الوقت (khawf fawt al-waqt): Fear that the prayer time will expire before one can perform wudu or ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position in this passage, what is the primary consideration for allowing tayammum?
A) Fear of missing the prayer time
B) The distance of travel
C) The availability of water
D) The presence of a funeral prayer

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits tayammum based on travel distance or physical absence of water, not on fear of missing the prayer time.
